  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d66e133e-bef5-4652-a168-8ba0b3dca82a'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13048154555218063621">Known for her popular blog, "The Book Whisperer," Donalyn Miller is a dedicated teacher who says she has yet to meet a child she couldn't turn into a reader. Her approach, however, is not conventional. Miller dispenses with the more traditional reading instruction of book reports and comprehension worksheets in favor of embracing students' choices in books and independent </span>
  <span id="freeText13048154555218063621" style="display:none">Known for her popular blog, "The Book Whisperer," Donalyn Miller is a dedicated teacher who says she has yet to meet a child she couldn't turn into a reader. Her approach, however, is not conventional. Miller dispenses with the more traditional reading instruction of book reports and comprehension worksheets in favor of embracing students' choices in books and independent reading. Her zeal for reading is infections and inspiring --and the results are remarkable. No matter how far behind Miller's students may be when they enter her 6th grade classroom, her students read an average of 40 books a year, achieve high scores on standardized tests, and internalize a love for books and reading that lasts long after they've left her class. Travel alongside the author as she leads her students to discover the ample rewards of reading and literature. Her secrets include:     <div>Affirming the reader in every student</div>    <div>Supporting students' reading choices</div>    <div>Carving out extra reading time</div>    <div>Modeling authentic reading behaviors</div>    <div>Discarding time-worn reading assisgnments</div>    <div>Developing a classroom library with high-interest books</div>    <p> Rich with classroom examples and practical advice and stitched together with the thread of Miller's passionate voice, this book will help teachers support students of all levels on their path to reading success and points a way out of the nation's literacy crisis. The book also includes an invaluable list of books that students most enjoy reading.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="13048154555218063621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/566654-has-anyone-tried-miller-s-method-of-free">Has anyone tried Miller&#39;s method of free choice in books with older students, like those in upper secondary school/sixth form college? More aspects of reading come into play when working with older students - developing analytical ability, talking about texts in groups, reading a canon etc. I wonder if free choice reading &quot;works&quot; with later curricular demands, so to speak.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_566654'><a id="like_count_community_question_566654" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/566654?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F4732276-the-book-whisperer">like</a></span>

</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/31539510-amy"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1486600549i/31539510._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/31539510-amy">Amy</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">A few on my teaching team have tried the free choice with students whom we know have a support system at home to help them navigate. For those with le<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">A few on my teaching team have tried the free choice with students whom we know have a support system at home to help them navigate. For those with less support, we encourage choosing from a range. We have wheeled book racks in the hallway outside of every class that are labeled by Running Record level. We point out where they can choose from &amp; we teach how to choose good fit books, and the three ways to read a book.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33347008" />
    <a title="Donalyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/829035-donalyn"><img alt="Donalyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577941818p2/829035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33347008?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donalyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donalyn" href="/user/show/829035-donalyn">Donalyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span> (Review from the author)

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">out of the box reading teachers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/829035-donalyn?shelf=adult-teaching-books">adult-teaching-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33347008" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2025610540127953799">OK, I only added this one to my list, so all of my GoodReads friends could see it!I could not believe that the book was already added to the GoodReads site...<br/><br/>The book will be published in March of 2009. I am currently doing the copy edits and the final tweaks on the production design. Still surreal to think that I wrote an entire book.<br/><br/>I think it is pretty good, but I am a bit biased. My husband teased me saying that it only deserved four stars out of five because he prefers books with action in </span>
  <span id="freeText2025610540127953799" style="display:none">OK, I only added this one to my list, so all of my GoodReads friends could see it!I could not believe that the book was already added to the GoodReads site...<br /><br />The book will be published in March of 2009. I am currently doing the copy edits and the final tweaks on the production design. Still surreal to think that I wrote an entire book.<br /><br />I think it is pretty good, but I am a bit biased. My husband teased me saying that it only deserved four stars out of five because he prefers books with action in them! I told him that if he had ever been in my Language Arts classroom, he would see plenty of action and adventure.<br /></span>

<div id="review_110536843" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110536843" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3968688-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391356908p2/3968688.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110536843?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/3968688-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110536843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3751854967490325605">This is a book I wish I could rate a couple of different ways. Because in some ways it is a 5 star book, and others it deserves a 2. Let me explain.<br/><br/>First why is it so great? I would highly suggest this for any reading/English teacher in America. There is nothing really complex about this book, but it certainly proposes a culture shift in reading education. The basic premise is that the way we are teaching reading is actually what is destroying the love of reading in America. Americans are readin</span>
  <span id="freeText3751854967490325605" style="display:none">This is a book I wish I could rate a couple of different ways. Because in some ways it is a 5 star book, and others it deserves a 2. Let me explain.<br /><br />First why is it so great? I would highly suggest this for any reading/English teacher in America. There is nothing really complex about this book, but it certainly proposes a culture shift in reading education. The basic premise is that the way we are teaching reading is actually what is destroying the love of reading in America. Americans are reading less and less all the time and it is this teacher/author's contention that we need to just let kids read instead of creating all the complex reading programs that we have in education. <br /><br />Miller requires her 6th graders to read 40 books during the school year! Not really surprising, many students meet this goal and exceed it and a byproduct of the students reading so much is their reading scores on the Texas state assessment actually improves. None of her students have failed the state assessment in four years and an average of 85% of her students score in the 90th percentile. There is no disputing she is doing something right.<br /><br />Why not so great? Ms. Miller has a bit of a God complex and it's not well placed at times. She is highly critical of everything we do in reading education. At times she writes as if she has all the answers to reading education and we should just follow her lead. Which leads us to a second criticism. At times you really don't understand how her classroom functions. We know she incorporates a lot of reading and time for her students to read, but we never get a solid picture of what her reading instruction looks like. Finally, her last chapter in this book is just total crap. She spends precious pages criticizing her fellow teachers in her district and really doing nothing to promote a collegial atmosphere. It seems that many teachers ruin all her hard work and she tells us how she gets severely distraught over this issue as well. This chapter just doesn't fit. She could have spent more time helping us to better understand her classroom. Because believe me, she gets your attention.<br /><br />The bottom line is this book really makes you think about the value of reading and really brings out the inner reader within you as a teacher.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3751854967490325605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_73517396" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73517396" />
    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/572977-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246380823p2/572977.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73517396?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/572977-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/572977-kate?shelf=nf">nf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/572977-kate?shelf=education">education</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73517396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1173313668204453676">Akin to Nancie Atwell's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7603.Reading_Lolita_in_Tehran_A_Memoir_in_Books' title='Reading Lolita in Tehran A Memoir in Books by Azar Nafisi' rel='nofollow noopener'>In the Middle</a> about reading workshop, Miller's book talks about creating a community of readers and encouraging students to read by giving them time in school to read. She points out that her students score well on their standardized tests, and she attributes this to her expectation that each student will read 40 books during their school year. I think she's right.<br/><br/>My only irritation with this book is that, as a school librarian, I find classroom libraries to be a connundr</span>
  <span id="freeText1173313668204453676" style="display:none">Akin to Nancie Atwell's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7603.Reading_Lolita_in_Tehran_A_Memoir_in_Books" title="Reading Lolita in Tehran A Memoir in Books by Azar Nafisi" rel="nofollow noopener">In the Middle</a> about reading workshop, Miller's book talks about creating a community of readers and encouraging students to read by giving them time in school to read. She points out that her students score well on their standardized tests, and she attributes this to her expectation that each student will read 40 books during their school year. I think she's right.<br /><br />My only irritation with this book is that, as a school librarian, I find classroom libraries to be a connundrum. They're GREAT! Kids need to have books available to them, right in front of them, they need to be able to easily abandon books and start others, talk about books, think about books, etc. However, I firmly believe that 1. The school library is not so far away that you can't send kids to it regularly and 2. schools are using the expectation of classroom libraries to diminish school library budgets and personnel. Miller says her classroom library is 2000+ volumes. My understocked school library is 7600. She says she buys books on the cheap. School librarians have CONNECTIONS. I get almost all my new paperbacks for at least 50% off cover price, and hardcovers for 40% off. I don't pay shipping and my books are cataloged so that it's even easier for kids to find what they're looking for than in a collection that is only known well by the teacher. In addition, school libraries can interlibrary loan materials so that each reader can have the books s/he really wants. When classroom teachers spend hundreds of dollars a year on books, I cringe. If I had that money, I could spend it more effectively to buy more materials that would be available to every kid in the school. <br /><br /> Teachers need to lobby for sufficient school library budgets so that good books are available in quantity for students. They need to lobby for sufficient qualified school library staff so that they can send kids to the library at any time to get new books, different books, etc. It frustrates me that even a teacher like Miller, who is a heavy library user, doesn't seem to know that there is a relationship between her spending her own money on books for her heavy-reading students and the school's measly budget for school library books. She talks about using the school's library, but insists that her classroom library is the main source for her students' reading material. If that's the case, I think something's wrong with her school library and librarian.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1173313668204453676" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_47942487" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/151597-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442480463p2/151597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47942487?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/151597-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47942487" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14704688199948484160">Donalyn Miller gets it. She understands perfectly why many of our kids don't like reading any more, and she has the answer. You'd think Congress would be knocking down her door by now. Let's hope it happens soon.<br/><br/>In the mean time, anyone who considers himself or herself a teacher needs to read THE BOOK WHISPERER. It's a book that gets right to the heart of what makes us readers and how to instill that love of words and stories in our kids. Miller goes right after so-called "tried and true" method</span>
  <span id="freeText14704688199948484160" style="display:none">Donalyn Miller gets it. She understands perfectly why many of our kids don't like reading any more, and she has the answer. You'd think Congress would be knocking down her door by now. Let's hope it happens soon.<br /><br />In the mean time, anyone who considers himself or herself a teacher needs to read THE BOOK WHISPERER. It's a book that gets right to the heart of what makes us readers and how to instill that love of words and stories in our kids. Miller goes right after so-called "tried and true" methods like comprehension tests, book reports, whole class required novels, and test preparation workbooks not just with empty criticism but with solid research that supports reading time and student choice. More importantly, she provides a healthy list of more kid-friendly, reading-friendly alternative strategies that teachers can use in their classrooms right away.<br /><br />Truly, this book is a model for getting kids back to books they love, and it provides a great model for classroom teachers to follow. For those who aren't sure where to start, there are plenty of anecdotes, sample student interactions, and useful classroom forms to get new teachers started.<br /><br />I'm both a children's author and a National Board Certified middle school English teacher, and I found myself nodding my way through these pages to the very end. Miller's ideas -- and they're ideas that smart teachers all over our country are using in various ways -- have the power to make a real difference in education.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14704688199948484160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_315573628" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/315573628" />
    <a title="Tatiana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3948070-tatiana"><img alt="Tatiana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578088534p2/3948070.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/315573628?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tatiana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tatiana" href="/user/show/3948070-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3948070-tatiana?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3948070-tatiana?shelf=education">education</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer315573628" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7202189430863977335"><i>“It has been said that teachers teach how they were taught.”</i> ~ P. 13<br/><br/>I think this statement is the crux of my bafflement and let down over <i>The Book Whisperer</i>. I would have been what Donalyn Miller calls an “Underground Reader” had it not been for the fact that I was homeschooled. I read and read growing up, and the line between books for school and for pleasure blurred. Every book was a “whole-class novel study” because I constituted the entire class! I guess I never doubted that I would allow my</span>
  <span id="freeText7202189430863977335" style="display:none"><i>“It has been said that teachers teach how they were taught.”</i> ~ P. 13<br /><br />I think this statement is the crux of my bafflement and let down over <i>The Book Whisperer</i>. I would have been what Donalyn Miller calls an “Underground Reader” had it not been for the fact that I was homeschooled. I read and read growing up, and the line between books for school and for pleasure blurred. Every book was a “whole-class novel study” because I constituted the entire class! I guess I never doubted that I would allow my own classroom of students to do the same: read and read and read, in different capacities and with varying outcomes and destinations, but still always reading. <br /><br />I will admit that there is good research concerning encouraging students to read within this volume. Reading instruction is necessary and valuable, but what develops good readers better than reading itself? Nothing. Kids need to read, many genres, and as often as possible. Period. Fact. What irked me about this memoir/instruction manual, and drove me to the point of discouragement, was Miller’s attitude that she is the very first teacher to ever stumble upon this idea in the history of education. It is basically a 200+ page plea for reading done her way, and that gets tired fast, especially when it all seems obvious.<br /><br />Believe me, I am against Accelerated Reading (AR) and other types of log-and-forget programs, too, but I have seen firsthand that whole-class novel studies, and literature circles, and reading groups can work. I have seen a read-aloud, novel study, and an independent reading period fill a fifth grade language arts block to great effect. You cannot forever let students read their own choices of books, because when they get to high school and hopefully college, there is assigned reading, whether they like the book or not. They will have to read what everyone else is reading or fail.<br /><br />Miller is also right in that the culture of testing and standards in American public schools is monopolizing. Is she calling for a reading revolution? Maybe. She’s going to have to be more specific about how she schedules her classroom hours if she is. I kept waiting for her to explain how she wove lessons on comprehension, fluency, vocabulary, and other reading strategies into this free-wheeling reading program, but it never came. The only accountability I could discern (mentioned towards the end of the book) was that the students write reviews and recommendations to post on the classroom blog. <br /><br />And what about elementary? It is one thing to dedicate so much time to independent reading when you’re solely teaching language arts to sixth graders, as Ms. Miller does. Early readers still require a lot of reading instruction, and their attention spans do not allow for 30-minute blocks of silent reading. (Trust me. You’re lucky if you get 10-15.) 90-minute literacy block or no, there has to be a balance of modeling, instruction, and independent work. That’s Teaching 101.<br /><br />That’s what I would say is missing, for me, from <i>The Book Whisperer</i>: balance. It is sad that so many teachers have switched to a classroom culture of test-prep. This is true. However, I do not feel it is fair to the students to provide no test prep at all. I will use one of my favorite adages in response to test-prep: <i>Don’t teach to the test. Teach how to take it.</i> Students can have lots of independent reading time while also learning how to successfully conquer the current education waters. Why can’t there be both?<br /><br />At the end of the day, getting kids to read is the prime objective, and Miller achieved that. Her students loved to read. I am all for encouraging life-long readers. I am one myself. I can’t understand people who don’t read. It is a fundamental part of how I have become the person I am now, traveling down the path toward the teacher I want to become. I agree that too many children are not the readers they need to be, some because of disinterest or learning disabilities or language barriers (Miller ignored accommodating exceptional students altogether); others because they were not given a good foundation of reading instruction when they needed it. What about these students who are struggling? Miller promotes giving them books at their reading level and eventually, they catch up. Independent reading is neither a catch-all, nor should it be in lieu of concentrated, individualized reading instruction. As she pointed out, one size does not fit all. After all, some of her students reached her 40-books-a-year goal and some of them didn’t. <br /><br />So much of what Miller was advocating was common sense to me, and I couldn’t stand how arrogant she was in touting this method, which is, in a nutshell, to model good reading habits and pay attention to the reading needs of your students. After this book, I am all the more thankful to my K-12 teacher (my mom) and my current education literacy professor. In their own ways, both have taught me that reading is the gateway to more learning. Learn to read so you can read to learn. Thanks to them, and my own hands-on research, I already believe reading is the most important skill that students will ever learn in their educational career. I don’t need The Book Whisperer herself or anyone else to convince me of that. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7202189430863977335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7205457824788845465" style="display:none">More than 20 years ago I began reading Lucy Calkins. I think the first book of hers I read was Living Between the Lines. From that moment I knew what I wanted my classroom to look like, to sound like, to be like. I knew I wanted to have a reading workshop &amp; a writing workshop and have great conversations with kids around reading and writing. I didn't know, though, how to actually start this. Then I read Ralph Fletcher's very user-friendly Writing Workshop. That got me started, and Lucy's Units of Study for Teaching Writing have helped me refine my teaching and learning. This book, The Book Whisperer, has done for my reading instruction what Fletcher's did for my writing instructtion. It consolidated all I thought and believed about reading (including Fountas and Pinnell's work, especially the Reader's Notebooks) and just pushed me right into implementing the program outlined by Donalyn Miller. The book is straight-forward and very easy to read. Thank you, Donalyn Miller, for changing my teaching life.</span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94124534" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13025187118120004099">Many years ago, I took a graduate class with the author, Donalyn Miller. The first day, our professor let everyone in the class know we had a reading celebrity in our midst. Many times, this professor would refer to Donalyn for her opinion. At that time, I decided I should probably read her book at some point. I purchased the book and stuck it on my to-read bookshelf. Like Donalyn, I have a mountain for my to-read list. During the school year, I read two things: professional pedagogy and researc</span>
  <span id="freeText13025187118120004099" style="display:none">Many years ago, I took a graduate class with the author, Donalyn Miller. The first day, our professor let everyone in the class know we had a reading celebrity in our midst. Many times, this professor would refer to Donalyn for her opinion. At that time, I decided I should probably read her book at some point. I purchased the book and stuck it on my to-read bookshelf. Like Donalyn, I have a mountain for my to-read list. During the school year, I read two things: professional pedagogy and research books for PhD. work and books for young adults. Either way, my choices are guided by what my needs are at that moment. So, this book had been relegated to the "one day" shelf until today. It is the summer, after all. <br /><br />What I learned by reading this book is that Donalyn's approach to teaching Reading is much like my own. I teach two subjects: English and Reading. In my Reading class, the focus is solidly on reading. We read independently 15-90 minutes per period. I do conferencing with my students, and spend hours trying to personally match books to students. I do this because I like to see what happens when a struggling reader suddenly becomes a stellar reader. When that happens, these same kids make immense jumps in their performance in other classes. Sometimes, this also means a change in their general behavior. All of a sudden, they have confidence. They feel affirmed. Apparently, Donalyn's experience with the importance of independent reading with 6th graders mirrors what I see at the high school level. In English, I focus most of my effort into writing while still maintaining an independent reading requirement. <br /><br />I appreciated the voice that Donalyn used, as it clearly spoke to an audience of teachers who care about reading. While providing big-name scholars research as a support for her viewpoint strengthens her argument, the passion that comes through leads to the truth of the matter even more. For Donalyn, reading is life. For me, this is also a reality that I try to express to others but can never quite seem to put it into words. I'm glad Donalyn has done it.<br /><br />Those people who like Donalyn's work should also check out Nancie Atwell's "In The Middle" and Penny Kittle's "Write Beside Them". </span>
  <a data-text-id="13025187118120004099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer38109539866195909">The thing I love most about the book is its focus: how to nurture our students to be enthusiastic readers. The stories of the struggles she had to help turn kids onto reading and the examples of things she's tried are incredibly helpful. This book is so practical and so helpful and, most importantly, so HOPEFUL.   <span id="freeText38109539866195909" style="display:none">The thing I love most about the book is its focus: how to nurture our students to be enthusiastic readers. The stories of the struggles she had to help turn kids onto reading and the examples of things she's tried are incredibly helpful. This book is so practical and so helpful and, most importantly, so HOPEFUL. I really needed that. <br /><br />I have to say that while I've always known part of my responsibility as a social studies teacher is to help my students learn to read text &amp; images I'd never really thought of myself as a reading teacher. I do now. I'm having some pretty great conversations with students during down time, asking them about what they're reading and pointing out some books I think they might like. It's so much fun!<br /><br />I highly recommend this book. If you can, read it with someone and discuss it. You'll have wonderful discussions and you'll dream big dreams about things you can do in your school. Every teacher should read it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="38109539866195909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5079126178107296634">I borrowed this book because I am an avid reader trying to inspire the same passion within my nephew. The beginning of the book offered some useful information about reader types, etc. Overall, I found the book to be more beneficial for teachers than a parent, guardian, etc. I felt the book was a tad idealistic and didn't really address those young readers who are adamant about not reading, especially if it means getting in the way of other games or activities.   <span id="freeText5079126178107296634" style="display:none">I borrowed this book because I am an avid reader trying to inspire the same passion within my nephew. The beginning of the book offered some useful information about reader types, etc. Overall, I found the book to be more beneficial for teachers than a parent, guardian, etc. I felt the book was a tad idealistic and didn't really address those young readers who are adamant about not reading, especially if it means getting in the way of other games or activities. I feel that forcing a child to read instead of playing games, etc. connects reading with punishment and pushes them even further away from the joys of reading. Another example is a child who becomes anxious when asked to read, because the struggle of new words is so disheartening to them. I've always allowed my nephew to choose the books he reads and he sees the passion I have for reading, but for some reason his interest ends with pictures -- I've even tried suggesting Iron Man comics and graphic novels.</span>

<div id="review_372364569" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/372364569" />
    <a title="Sally" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7244049-sally"><img alt="Sally" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361742409p2/7244049.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/372364569?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sally" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sally" href="/user/show/7244049-sally">Sally</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780470623428" href="/book/show/7656396-the-book-whisperer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7244049-sally?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7244049-sally?shelf=cover-to-cover-2012">cover-to-cover-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7244049-sally?shelf=work-related">work-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7244049-sally?shelf=to-read-in-2012">to-read-in-2012</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer372364569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12895852997102714144"><b>DEFINITELY BEYOND 5 STARS...WOW.</b> So Donalyn Miller did for me what no other professional development book ever has - it has made me want to leave administration and actually become a reading/language arts teacher. I have been in education for 25 years and have cringed at teaching language arts, not because I don't adore reading (which I do), but because I hate group reads, anthologies, and worksheets that students spend more time on than actual reading! <br/><br/>Immediately after finishing this book, I s</span>
  <span id="freeText12895852997102714144" style="display:none"><b>DEFINITELY BEYOND 5 STARS...WOW.</b> So Donalyn Miller did for me what no other professional development book ever has - it has made me want to leave administration and actually become a reading/language arts teacher. I have been in education for 25 years and have cringed at teaching language arts, not because I don't adore reading (which I do), but because I hate group reads, anthologies, and worksheets that students spend more time on than actual reading! <br /><br />Immediately after finishing this book, I spent a considerable time texting with my academic coach in which I recommended she read it pronto. She and I are of the same belief and I know she'll embrace the concept as much as I do. Then, how can we implement the concept at our site???? <br /><br />What Donalyn does, though, is remarkable. She speaks to the simplicity of her "allowing students to read" concept, but downplays some very critical points - 1) She is a dedicated teacher who obviously spends multiple hours not only pouring over the plethora of books her students might read, but to review and comment in their notebooks and to make suggestions? That takes serious time beyond the normal life of even a great teacher. Add to that the time spent finding books and preparing them for library use. 2) She is a gifted person herself. You cannot make these recommendations lightly - these are students. She knows her literature and is a true expert in her field. We need more teachers like her that are truly gifted. I know of a handful that might be right there with her, and they'll be the first that I recommend this book to. 3) She has the ultimate and complete support of her family. It takes this support to not only spend every spare cent on school supplies/books (as I know all teachers do), but to invest the time engrossed in a book is a culture that her family supports and, I'm sure, participates in themselves. I can only imagine the "book club" types of discussions that go on at the dinner table.<br /><br />Mrs. Miller - I commend you and give you my highest rating, including being on my favorites shelf. Well done.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12895852997102714144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2082593549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13372949975150815542">I am so glad I discovered this book and based on the first 2 chapters decided to assign it for my fall reading course for my Education Grad students. Miller describes how she gets her 6th grade kids to read - even those who don't like to read. She waits until the last two chapters to debunk many current practices including book talks, book logs, entire classes reading the same book and more. You name a current practice, and she pretty much dismisses them as ineffective. The one practice that she</span>
  <span id="freeText13372949975150815542" style="display:none">I am so glad I discovered this book and based on the first 2 chapters decided to assign it for my fall reading course for my Education Grad students. Miller describes how she gets her 6th grade kids to read - even those who don't like to read. She waits until the last two chapters to debunk many current practices including book talks, book logs, entire classes reading the same book and more. You name a current practice, and she pretty much dismisses them as ineffective. The one practice that she pushes that has fallen out of favor is giving student time to read in class. Simple, but powerful. But this isn't the old Drop Everything And Read time. Miller spends a lot of time getting to know her students, and then figures out books they will like. By matching students to books, she makes it easy - the students can't wait to read.<br />Highly recommended for teacher educators, and K-12 educators, as well as parents.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13372949975150815542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1638276978" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1638276978" />
    <a title="Corey Wozniak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17339120-corey-wozniak"><img alt="Corey Wozniak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360530485p2/17339120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1638276978?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corey Wozniak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corey Wozniak" href="/user/show/17339120-corey-wozniak">Corey Wozniak</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1638276978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7422581159221142931">Donalyn Miller is a soldier in the fight to make reading pleasurable again. Schools have hijacked reading, but "Reading ultimately belongs to readers, not schools, and not schoolteachers." Miller argues that thoughtful teachers should tear down the walls erected between students and books (book reports, worksheets, whole-class novels, incentive programs, Accelerated Reader, book logs, comprehension tests, and other miscellaneous drudgery) and instead reacquaint students with the joys of reading </span>
  <span id="freeText7422581159221142931" style="display:none">Donalyn Miller is a soldier in the fight to make reading pleasurable again. Schools have hijacked reading, but "Reading ultimately belongs to readers, not schools, and not schoolteachers." Miller argues that thoughtful teachers should tear down the walls erected between students and books (book reports, worksheets, whole-class novels, incentive programs, Accelerated Reader, book logs, comprehension tests, and other miscellaneous drudgery) and instead reacquaint students with the joys of reading by letting them select their own books and giving them time in class to read. Students should discover for themselves, with teachers as their guide, that reading is its own reward, not merely a means to an end (test scores, prizes). <br /><br />Miller's strategy focuses on the long-game; she is focused on making kids LIFE-LONG readers. She suggests suggests we take a critical look at traditional reading instructional practices ("unexamined wallpaper") that use external carrot-and-stick motivations to get kids to read--once these motivations are taken away, the child will stop reading. Instead, plant in their heart the joy of reading and they'll continue to read regardless of external conditions. <br /><br />I'm on D. Miller's team, wholeheartedly. I give this book 4*s only because reading it wasn't the *holy conversion experience* that reading Nancie Atwell's <i> The Reading Zone </i> was for me last year. I've implemented most of Miller's suggestions already this year, and there wasn't too much I hadn't already considered since implementing "The Reading Zone" in my classroom. (The book was actually probably a 3* experience for me, because I was already a converted acolyte to the religion of free voluntary reading and much of the book was preaching to the choir. But for the sake of solidarity, I'll reward it 4*s.) Still, there were some unique contributions: Miller dedicates a whole chapter on the need for teachers to develop their own identities as readers in order to serve as a reading role model for students. This is an important and under-appreciated aspect to literacy instruction, and I'm glad she covered it.<br /><br />Another thing I took away was the importance of maximizing reading opportunities in the classroom. Miller, for example, doesn't do "bell-ringers". "Bell-ringers" are space-holders, she argues, and have little instructional benefit. Instead, kids enter the classroom and read for the "bell-ringer." A cool idea that I will consider for next year.<br /><br />And a quote to take us away: <br />"Books are immortality for writers, and as the conduit through which books have flowed into the hands of so many children, I feel that books are my immortality, too."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7422581159221142931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sirpa Grierson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sirpa Grierson" href="/user/show/1002825-sirpa-grierson">Sirpa Grierson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200754687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16795531997592609482">Loved this book. Donalyn Miller is practical and honest in her recounting of the struggles she had in getting her class to read a book. Then with a little "tweak" her students now read dozens of books each year. Her method reminds me of my 6th and 7th grade teacher, Mr. Gibson, a once-in-a-lifetime model of great teaching who somehow came from Glasgow, Scotland to our little provincial Elementary school in White Rock, British Columbia, Canada. Like the author, he allowed us to spend hours readin</span>
  <span id="freeText16795531997592609482" style="display:none">Loved this book. Donalyn Miller is practical and honest in her recounting of the struggles she had in getting her class to read a book. Then with a little "tweak" her students now read dozens of books each year. Her method reminds me of my 6th and 7th grade teacher, Mr. Gibson, a once-in-a-lifetime model of great teaching who somehow came from Glasgow, Scotland to our little provincial Elementary school in White Rock, British Columbia, Canada. Like the author, he allowed us to spend hours reading and discussing literature. We started off reading Nancy Drew and other series books, but slowly were absorbed into more difficult texts that still astonish me. During my years with him, our entire class became readers--Thomas Hardy, Charles Dickens, Jane Austen and the Bronte sisters, were all among the classics which became our friends in a very real way--as we each devoured books under his watchful eye. Frank Smith said that we need to introduce children into the "reading club" by a simple recipe, "the more you read, the better you read; the better you read, the more you read." Worksheets, drills, and boring assessments will never do what reading widely voraciously, and through one's own volition, will. When will grownups wake up to the fact that reading must be caught, not taught? I have always contended that if reading were made a subversive activity, every child in America would be reading at night with a flashlight under their covers. :)<br /><br />I have several friends I have recommended this book to and have heard that their students are reading like never before. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16795531997592609482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17216626898674886889">This is the best professional book that I have read in quite some time. It is so important to help children find the book that will turn them into lifelong readers. I reflected heavily on my own education and upbringing and came to the conclusion that I am an avid reader because of my mom and a few teachers that really kept me excited about reading.   <span id="freeText17216626898674886889" style="display:none">This is the best professional book that I have read in quite some time. It is so important to help children find the book that will turn them into lifelong readers. I reflected heavily on my own education and upbringing and came to the conclusion that I am an avid reader because of my mom and a few teachers that really kept me excited about reading. I was able to recall a high school math teacher (I really had a rough time in math) that approached me in the library (that’s what they called it in my day) and struck up a conversation about historical fiction. I didn’t learn to love math, but I finally saw her as a person. My brother struggled with reading and did not become a “reader” until well into his adult years. I think that all of the “kill and drill” that he had to endure made reading a chore rather that a desirable thing to do.<br /><br /><br /><br />I highly recommend this book for every teacher – even those math teachers! Donalyn Miller relates many strategies that have made her students successful in becoming readers as well as performing very well on standardized tests. This one is worth your while!<br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17765989452729557814"> Miller dispenses with the more traditional reading instruction of book reports and comprehension worksheets in favor of embracing students' choices in books and independent reading. Her zeal for reading is infectious and inspiring --and the results are remarkable. No matter how far behind Miller's students may be when they enter her 6th grade classroom, her students read an average of 40 books a year, achieve high scores on standardized tests, and internalize a love for books and reading that l</span>
  <span id="freeText17765989452729557814" style="display:none"> Miller dispenses with the more traditional reading instruction of book reports and comprehension worksheets in favor of embracing students' choices in books and independent reading. Her zeal for reading is infectious and inspiring --and the results are remarkable. No matter how far behind Miller's students may be when they enter her 6th grade classroom, her students read an average of 40 books a year, achieve high scores on standardized tests, and internalize a love for books and reading that lasts long after they've left her class. <br /><br />I totally believe Miller's philosophy and buy into the idea that kids need to be reading books that interest them to truly become life long readers. I just didn't quite get what this looks like in a typical classroom. I would have liked more detail about how she does the instructional part of her reading program. <br /><br />My district and school are committed to a basal program and expect all teachers to use it, so I am having a hard time implementing Miller's ideas in my own 4th grade classroom. But I am trying to have independent reading time--and hopefully I can get my students more interested in reading.</span>

<div id="review_445526288" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/445526288" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/881570-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220764862p2/881570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445526288?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/881570-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/881570-patrick?shelf=education">education</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/881570-patrick?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445526288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15468906540854797054">OK, very mixed feelings about this book. I came in expecting to love it since I had made some positive changes to my class just from attending the workshop of another teacher who had read it. Helping kids love to read is a goal right up my alley. But I ended up loving much of the goal, but not the inaccurate comparisons and denunciations of other teachers.<br/><br/>The positive: I totally agree with the author's basic premise of trying to help kids awaken their inner curiosity and become autonomous reader</span>
  <span id="freeText15468906540854797054" style="display:none">OK, very mixed feelings about this book. I came in expecting to love it since I had made some positive changes to my class just from attending the workshop of another teacher who had read it. Helping kids love to read is a goal right up my alley. But I ended up loving much of the goal, but not the inaccurate comparisons and denunciations of other teachers.<br /><br />The positive: I totally agree with the author's basic premise of trying to help kids awaken their inner curiosity and become autonomous readers and learners. Love it! I have done some of these things throughout my career, but improved since the workshop on what another teacher is doing in regards to this book. Reading the whole book for myself has also given me ideas for improving even more this year and next. I do well on the informal part of this--recommending books and getting many kids excited about them, but want to add the subtle formal supports that get them reading entire books, reflecting on them, and sharing them. I also want to develop more assignments that involve the books they are reading on their own. <br /><br />Many of the recommendations have to be adjusted for a jr. high schedule--time and requirements are different than elementary school--but the core ideals can be integrated in many ways.<br /><br />BUT, the negative: I just feel the author had a consistently arrogant tone that detracts from her message: "Trust your students, but not any other teacher or your own opinion if it differs from mine." I feel she would be hard to work with as a colleague. I think she projects her own defensive attitude onto the other teachers when she keeps saying she could never discuss differences of opinions with them. I don't see public education as this dreary world of cookie cutter units with boring texts that she insists is the norm. Texas has had its crazy testing regime in place since the mid 90's, so maybe she has seen some teachers forced into some boring things by the system. I also treat test reading merely as a genre like she does.<br /><br />Also, while I loved her openness with the kids about her goals, I would bet money that some of the comments she shares from students about how much they hate other classes and any novel read together in class stem from them parroting her own negative comments back at her. How does she frame their book reading? From this text, it seems very likely that she's repeating her harsh assessments of everyone else to her students as part of her instruction. Her classifications of the readers as always in conflict with teachers are as negative as the ones she rejects.<br /><br />I think class novels can actually complement individual reading and open up new things to students. I also think it was personally valuable to be "forced" to read some novels I didn't like. (But not all. Good Earth. Uuuuugh. I'm with you there Ms. Miller.) I can see that maybe that is more of a high school or honors class thing because we were already readers anyway, but I still feel there's a place for being introduced to new texts. She may actually be poisoning the well for future teachers who do engaging and important things with classroom novels. I do some of this in class, so take this for what it's worth with my biases. <br /><br />I love the novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26480.Witness" title="Witness by Karen Hesse" rel="nofollow noopener">Witness</a> that I read with the 7th graders, and I strongly support the 9th graders reading To Kill a Mockingbird and Romeo and Juliet. I think many students lives and sense of empathy are powerfully changed by reading To Kill a Mockingbird, and I think that the majority of them would not read it on their own. I would not have read it on my own while in jr. high or high school.<br /><br />So I think you have to take the great good in what Ms. Miller advocates, but with a healthy handful of salt. She is not God's gift to 6th graders and all other teachers.<br /><br />I would be interested in what other people's experiences in school were like--especially with all the different preferences of subject matter. As students, did they feel the same way the author does and just forced and bored all the time? If so, maybe I am just blinded by my own reading biases and "wallpaper" practices.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15468906540854797054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_377234347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/377234347" />
    <a title="Carrie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9783165-carrie-g"><img alt="Carrie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343277618p2/9783165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/377234347?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carrie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carrie G" href="/user/show/9783165-carrie-g">Carrie G</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer377234347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12084386891088120729">“The Book Whisperer” by Donalyn Miller was amazing! It is not a teen fiction book, but one of the many “teacher books” I hope to read this summer. Throughout the book I felt affirmed, validated, and (on more than one occasion) guilty. But, as a reading teacher and bibliophile, it was wonderful to find a book that spoke my language!<br/><br/>In “The Book Whisperer” Donalyn Miller, a 6th grade teacher from Texas, shares her experiences and insights related to independent reading-based reading instruction. M</span>
  <span id="freeText12084386891088120729" style="display:none">“The Book Whisperer” by Donalyn Miller was amazing! It is not a teen fiction book, but one of the many “teacher books” I hope to read this summer. Throughout the book I felt affirmed, validated, and (on more than one occasion) guilty. But, as a reading teacher and bibliophile, it was wonderful to find a book that spoke my language!<br /><br />In “The Book Whisperer” Donalyn Miller, a 6th grade teacher from Texas, shares her experiences and insights related to independent reading-based reading instruction. Mrs. Miller states that her primary goal as a reading teacher is to create life-long readers, children who will fall in love with books now and become adults that remain in love with books. She is adamant that the way to do this is NOT through basal reading series, drill-and-kill comprehension worksheets, reading incentive programs, whole-class novels, or standardized test practice. She believes that allowing/requiring the students to read self-selected novels from a wide variety of genres everyday in class is the way to accomplish this goal. The added bonus is that, with a little purposeful instruction, students will become proficient readers who are able to meet the requirements of standardized tests.<br /><br />First, I was blown away when this woman said that she has a classroom library of 2,000 books!!! 2,000 books, I just can’t imagine it! I felt like I had a pretty good-sized library in my room until I read this book. I feel quite inadequate now. On top of that, this full-time teacher and mother still manages to read a book a week during the school year – she reads extra over breaks and says she reads about 100 books total each year!!! I thought the 70 (total) that I hope to have done by the end of summer break was impressive… again, I feel inadequate!<br /><br />I’m not sure whether my students will be glad I read this book or not. Mrs. Miller did advocate against booktalks (which many will be happy to hear), but she advocates for book reviews (which many will NOT be happy to hear). She believes in giving students time to read EVERY DAY in class for 20 – 30 minutes (which many students will be happy to hear), but that is because she requires her students to read 40 books each year (which many will NOT be happy to hear). Mrs. Miller advocates for student-selected, independent reading (which many students will be happy to hear), but she also advocates for students to read a set number of books from each of a variety of genres (which many will NOT be happy to hear). I think my students would give this book mixed reviews.<br /><br />What I am walking away from this book with are:<br /><br />1) “You cannot inspire others to do what you are not inspired to do yourself.” I need to be more inspired to read every day, all kinds of books, so that my students can be even more inspired to read. <br /><br />2) “We make time for what we value, and if we value reading, we must make time for it.” I need to do better at showing the students that I value reading by giving them time DURING CLASS to read. <br /><br />3) Discuss more, assign less. Discussions about books are a natural result of reading and are what adults do when they finish a book. Worksheets, projects, and tests are not “real world” – they are not what real readers do at the end of a book. <br /><br />4) Set expectations HIGH! Why not require the students to read 40 books a year? Why not bring back the genre reading requirements? <br /><br />If you're a teacher who feels like your love for books may be bordering on a mental illness, pick up "The Book Whisperer" today. You'll be validated, uplifted, and renewed. What a great way to start another school year!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12084386891088120729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_108140452" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/108140452" />
    <a title="Tenille Shade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3906570-tenille-shade"><img alt="Tenille Shade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373140598p2/3906570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/108140452?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tenille Shade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tenille Shade" href="/user/show/3906570-tenille-shade">Tenille Shade</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3906570-tenille-shade?shelf=professional-reading">professional-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer108140452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9614115000021357262">This book will probably be the one professional development resource I hang onto for a lifetime. Donalyn Miller wasn’t preaching a new message, but it sure fell fresh on ears that needed to hear it! As I read this book, images of my 3rd grade teacher, Mrs. Barnes, flashed through my head continually. She lived and breathed authentic reading in her classroom, and she is the reason I am a voracious reader today. The author explains that teachers should always have a book in their hand and a recomm</span>
  <span id="freeText9614115000021357262" style="display:none">This book will probably be the one professional development resource I hang onto for a lifetime. Donalyn Miller wasn’t preaching a new message, but it sure fell fresh on ears that needed to hear it! As I read this book, images of my 3rd grade teacher, Mrs. Barnes, flashed through my head continually. She lived and breathed authentic reading in her classroom, and she is the reason I am a voracious reader today. The author explains that teachers should always have a book in their hand and a recommendation on their lips. I want to be that kind of teacher!<br /><br />My favorite suggestion in the book is to teach my students how to “steal” reading time. I am definitely going to train my 4th graders to grab a book anytime there is a classroom interruption. Surveying the kids reading interest at the beginning of the year is a good idea, because it will help me collect book stacks to place on their desk with a sticky note encouraging them to pick one they like. I love the idea of having a “book frenzy” at the beginning of the year where we dump all of our books on the floor and the students rifle through them trying to decide how we should organize our classroom library. I will definitely be scheduling library time this year!<br /><br />One of the most profound changes I hope to make this year is the book requirement. I’ve always believed in giving my students choice, but I’ve struggled to find a way to give them structures to help them explore several genres of literature. I want to utilize the Reader’s Notebook more, and a one a week letter seems reasonable. I’d also like my kids to do “book commercials” on my classroom blog. <br /><br />Near the end of the book, I started to feel like Donalyn Miller could read my mind. She writes about the importance of letting go and turning the keys over to her students. Trust is such a huge issue for control freak teachers like me! I was thankful to know that she still panics a little when the other language arts teachers start shoving TAKS practices down their kid’s throats. Thankfully that still, small voice whispers deep inside of her, “You’re kids are ready for this thing. And more importantly, they are going to be lifelong readers!” She points out that it shouldn’t take a capable reader 6 hours to complete a reading test and that the best method for building up reading miles is to read books. <br /><br />I was challenged by this book in so many ways. I want to become a change agent that fights for authentic reading experiences. I want to model the love of reading for my kids and show them what it means to live out what I believe. I am going to challenge myself to read at least 1 children’s book each week, and I plan to keep a record of my reading life from this point forward. This response I’ve written today is only the beginning!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9614115000021357262" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2882053833" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2882053833" />
    <a title="Ensiform" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform"><img alt="Ensiform" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275973241p2/3843117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2882053833?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ensiform" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ensiform" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform">Ensiform</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=work">work</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2882053833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4589421659305021434">[Random Read. 34, Work Related.]<br/><br/>The author, some sort of reading guru and blogger, explains how she develops a genuine love of reading in her students by making them participants in their reading development and putting books in their hands. She discusses how the typical, old-fashioned teaching methods of whole-class novels, comprehension worksheets, and vocabulary instruction fail to ignite any passion for reading and often have the opposite result. She describes her own journey of discovery in</span>
  <span id="freeText4589421659305021434" style="display:none">[Random Read. 34, Work Related.]<br /><br />The author, some sort of reading guru and blogger, explains how she develops a genuine love of reading in her students by making them participants in their reading development and putting books in their hands. She discusses how the typical, old-fashioned teaching methods of whole-class novels, comprehension worksheets, and vocabulary instruction fail to ignite any passion for reading and often have the opposite result. She describes her own journey of discovery in the classroom, from trying those methods and seeing poor results, to realizing that worksheets and vocabulary do not connect students with books. She decided that what does connect kids with books is putting books in the hands of students and discussing them. She also gives the students responsibility and rights, such as the freedom to choose which books they read and the freedom to drop a book if they don't like it. Any genre, any type, as long as the students themselves have some kind of ownership of the process.<br /><br />As with every zealot with an philosophy to teach, whether promoting snake oil, daily exercise, or classroom strategies, Miller suffers from the faults of the zealot: blinders to obstacles and an absolute faith that this will work (in her telling, every student becomes a devoted believer). It won't cure all ills, of course, but this book is a helpful, straightforward guide to connecting kids with reading in an organic, natural way. I myself have long used book partners in the classroom, which is a great way to spark reading interest in the reluctant readers (kids who haven't had positive experience with books) and dormant readers (gifted readers bored with whole-class instruction) that Miller describes. I had a few reservations about her system, of course: it requires knowledge of a wide variety of novels at a variety of reading levels, knowledge of the students' levels, and a lot of organization. That doesn't just happen in the first week of a school year. Objections aside, school boards and mandated district testing unfortunately don't easily lend themselves to Miller's methodology. The bottom line is that Miller's claims of meeting a 40-book-a-year goal for every student are enviable, and should be emulated, but probably in practice can't. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4589421659305021434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
